#### You said:

Recourses establishment

  

#### ChatGPT said:

**Resource establishment** is a planning phase where you identify, secure, and organize the **resources required to operate a system, project, or business** before it begins running.

In practical terms, it answers one question:

**“What do we need, and how do we make sure it exists before operations start?”**

---

## 1\. Main Categories of Resources

### 1\. Physical Resources

These are **tangible assets**.

Examples:

- Land
- Buildings
- Equipment
- Vehicles
- Pipes, pumps, tanks
- Computers and servers

Example for a **fish farm**:

- Tanks
- Pumps
- Biofilters
- Oxygen systems
- Backup generators

---

### 2\. Human Resources

The **people required to run the operation**.

Examples:

- Managers
- Technicians
- Farm workers
- Engineers
- Sales staff
- Support staff

Key factors:

- Skills required
- Training
- Staffing levels
- Labour cost

---

### 3\. Financial Resources

The **capital needed to build and sustain the operation**.

Examples:

- Startup capital
- Operating capital
- Credit lines
- Grants
- Investor funding

Includes:

- CAPEX (capital expenditure)
- OPEX (operating expenses)

---

### 4\. Information Resources

The **knowledge and systems needed to run the operation**.

Examples:

- SOPs
- Software
- Databases
- Market data
- Monitoring systems
- Automation tools

Example:

- Farm monitoring dashboards
- Pricing databases
- AI automation systems

---

### 5\. Supply Chain Resources

Everything required to **maintain inputs and outputs**.

Examples:

- Feed suppliers
- Seed suppliers
- Packaging
- Distribution partners
- Transport systems

---

## 2\. Steps in Resource Establishment

### Step 1 — Define Operational Requirements

What does the system need to function?

Example:

- A trout RAS facility producing **24 tons/year**.

---

### Step 2 — Calculate Resource Requirements

Determine quantities.

Example:

- Water volume
- Pump capacity
- Tank numbers
- Staff numbers
- Feed demand

---

### Step 3 — Source Resources

Find suppliers or providers.

Examples:

- Equipment manufacturers
- Staffing agencies
- Technology vendors
- Financial institutions

---

### Step 4 — Allocate Resources

Assign resources to specific functions.

Example:

| Function | Resource |
| --- | --- |
| Fish production | Tanks + filtration |
| Processing | Packhouse |
| Sales | Distribution vehicles |
| Monitoring | Sensors + software |

---

### Step 5 — Create Redundancy

Critical systems require backups.

Example in aquaculture:

- Backup pumps
- Backup oxygen
- Backup power

---

## 3\. Example: Resource Establishment for an Aquaponic Farm

| Resource Type | Example |
| --- | --- |
| Land | 1 hectare |
| Infrastructure | Greenhouses + tanks |
| Equipment | Pumps, blowers, filters |
| Staff | 1 manager + 3 workers |
| Technology | Monitoring software |
| Supply chain | Seed + feed suppliers |
| Finance | Startup capital |

---

## 4\. Common Mistake in Resource Establishment

Most operations fail because they **underestimate operational resources**.

Typical mistakes:

- Understaffing
- Undersized equipment
- No redundancy
- No working capital buffer

A rule used in engineering-heavy industries:

**Operational resources should have a 20–40% buffer capacity.**

---

## 5\. Strategic Resource Establishment

At a higher level (for large systems or franchises), resources are structured into layers:

1. **Core infrastructure**
2. **Operational systems**
3. **Automation and software**
4. **Management structure**
5. **Financial backing**

This is how scalable companies are built.

---

## 6\. In Your Case (Your Businesses)

You are actually building **three different resource systems simultaneously**:

1. **Aquaponic farm infrastructure**
2. **Consulting / franchise development**
3. **AI automation systems for business**

Each of these requires a **separate resource establishment plan**.

---

If you want, I can also show you something more useful:

**The “Resource Stack” needed to build a scalable aquaponic franchise system.**

That model will show you the **exact resources needed to scale farms internationally**, which aligns with your long-term generational wealth plan.

#### You said:

I have a uncaped internet at home and i would like to host my own websites and webapps for YBAsystems untill my laptop cant handle the laod then ill or should i go straight to hostin g or buying cheap second hand server

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Start simple. Do **not** buy a server yet.

Your limiting factor at home will **not be internet**, it will be:

- **Power outages**
- **Router/NAT configuration**
- **Security**
- **Hardware uptime**
- **Static IP issues**

A laptop can run a surprising amount of infrastructure if you structure it correctly.

---

## Option 1 — Host on Your Laptop (Best Starting Point)

For early development of **YBASystems / YBAFlow / web apps**, your laptop is sufficient.

What you need:

**Software stack**

- Ubuntu or Linux VM
- Docker
- Docker Compose
- Nginx or Traefik
- PostgreSQL
- n8n (for automations)
- Git

**Advantages**

- Cost = **R0**
- Full control
- Perfect for development
- Easy to rebuild

You can run **10–20 small web apps** without problems.

---

## The Real Problem: Home Network Access

Most home connections sit behind **CGNAT**.

This means your server **cannot be accessed from the internet directly**.

Solutions:

### Option A — Cloudflare Tunnel (Best)

Use **Cloudflare Zero Trust tunnels**.

Benefits:

- No port forwarding
- No static IP needed
- Free
- Secure

Your architecture becomes:

Internet  
   ↓  
Cloudflare  
   ↓  
Tunnel  
   ↓  
Your Laptop Server

---

### Option B — Cheap VPS Reverse Proxy

Rent a **R80–R150/month VPS**.

Example providers:

- Hetzner
- Contabo
- Hostinger
- DigitalOcean

Architecture:

Internet  
   ↓  
VPS (public server)  
   ↓  
Wireguard tunnel  
   ↓  
Home server

This is very scalable.

---

## Option 2 — Cheap Second-Hand Server

Do **not** rush here.

Used servers consume **massive electricity**.

Example:

Old enterprise server:

Power draw: 200–500W  
Monthly electricity (SA):  
R600 – R1500

That is **more expensive than cloud hosting**.

---

## Option 3 — Mini Server (Better than Used Enterprise)

If you want local infrastructure later, buy something like:

- **HP EliteDesk Mini**
- **Dell Optiplex Micro**
- **Lenovo ThinkCentre Tiny**

Typical specs:

i5 / i7  
32GB RAM  
1TB SSD  
Power usage: 15–40W

Cost used in SA:

R4000 – R7000

These make **excellent home servers**.

---

## What I Would Do (Practical Strategy)

Stage 1 — Development

Run everything on your laptop.

Stack:

Ubuntu  
Docker  
PostgreSQL  
n8n  
Redis  
Nginx

Expose via:

**Cloudflare Tunnel**

Cost: **R0**

---

Stage 2 — Small Production

Buy a **cheap VPS**

Example:

2 CPU  
4GB RAM  
80GB SSD

Cost:

R100–R200/month

Run:

- websites
- APIs
- automation

---

Stage 3 — Scale

Move heavier systems to cloud or dedicated machines.

---

## Important Reality

Running servers from home is **fine for development**.

It is **not reliable for production businesses** because of:

- Load shedding
- Internet interruptions
- Hardware failure

---

## My Recommendation for YBASystems

Use a **hybrid model**:

Home Server  
Development + AI agents  
  
VPS  
Public websites  
API endpoints  
Customer apps

Cheap, scalable, safe.

---

If you want, I can also show you something extremely useful:

**The $10/month architecture used by many AI automation agencies to host unlimited client automations.**

It will fit perfectly with what you're building with **n8n, Snowie, and WhatsApp automation.**

  

#### You said:

ok im going for the the hostinger kvm2 plan tell me what is the best stack i need ubuntu what os should i use i have experience with cpanel but im willling to learn somthing new and what applications should i add bare in mant i need scalable safe and open claw as well as claud code

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Good choice. **Hostinger KVM 2 (2 vCPU / 8 GB RAM / 100 GB NVMe / 8 TB)** is enough to host multiple sites + a couple of small apps if you keep the stack clean. [Hostinger+1](https://www.hostinger.com/vps-hosting?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

### OS choice (don’t overthink it)

Use **Ubuntu Server 24.04 LTS** unless you have a hard reason not to. It’s stable, widely supported, and works well with Docker-first stacks. If a specific tool you want is picky, Ubuntu **22.04 LTS** is the safe fallback (very common in tutorials/docs). [DigitalOcean+1](https://www.digitalocean.com/community/tutorials/deploy-application-coolify?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

---

## The best “scalable + safe” stack for what you described

You’re coming from cPanel, so you want something that gives you:

- simple deployments
- TLS handled automatically
- easy rollbacks
- good security defaults
- Docker support for OpenClaw + “Claude Code” tooling

### Core foundation (base server)

1. **SSH keys only** (no password login)
2. **UFW firewall** (allow only 22, 80, 443)
3. **fail2ban** (basic brute-force protection)
4. **unattended-upgrades** (security updates)
5. **automatic backups** (daily off-server)

This is the boring stuff that prevents pain later.

---

## App/deployment layer (cPanel replacement)

### Recommended: **Coolify**

Think of Coolify as “self-hosted Vercel/Heroku-lite” for Docker and Git-based deploys.

Why it fits you:

- Works great with **Docker** apps
- Handles **reverse proxy + HTTPS**
- Lets you deploy multiple projects cleanly
- Easier than manually wiring Nginx configs forever

Coolify is actively documented for Ubuntu 22.04+ and installs with Docker-based setup. [Coolify+1](https://coolify.io/docs/get-started/installation?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

**Alternative if you want simpler:** CapRover (also good). But I’d start with Coolify.

---

## Reverse proxy & TLS

If you use Coolify, it will manage this.  
If you don’t use Coolify, choose one:

- **Traefik** (best with Docker labels)
- **Caddy** (very simple, auto HTTPS)

---

## Databases & queues (keep these standard)

Run these as Docker services (separate from apps):

- **PostgreSQL** (main database)
- **Redis** (queues, caching, sessions)

Do **not** expose Postgres/Redis to the public internet. Only internal Docker network.

---

## “Ops” apps you should add early (small footprint, big value)

- **Portainer** (optional) – quick container visibility
- **Uptime Kuma** – uptime monitoring & alerts
- **Plausible** (optional) – analytics (self-hosted)
- **Log rotation** + disk monitoring (KVM2 disk is finite)

---

# Where OpenClaw and Claude Code fit

## OpenClaw

OpenClaw has an official **Docker install path**, so treat it like any other containerized app. [OpenClaw](https://docs.openclaw.ai/install/docker?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

**Security note (important):** OpenClaw has had recent security issues reported (weak local auth / malicious “skills” ecosystem), so do not expose it casually and be strict about what extensions/skills you install. Keep it behind auth/VPN, and update quickly. [TechRadar+2The Verge+2](https://www.techradar.com/pro/security/a-human-chosen-password-doesnt-stand-a-chance-openclaw-has-yet-another-major-security-flaw-heres-what-we-know-about-clawjacked?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

Practical approach:

- Run OpenClaw on a **private subdomain** (e.g., `claw.ybasystems.com`)
- Put it behind:
	- Coolify auth (if available) or
		- Cloudflare Access / basic auth / VPN

## Claude Code

Claude Code has its own setup docs and requirements; treat it like a **developer tool** that runs on the server only if you truly need it there. [Claude+1](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/setup?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

My advice:

- Run Claude Code mainly on your **local machine** for development.
- Only install on VPS if you’re doing server-side workflows (CI, repo agents, etc.).

---

# The stack I’d install on Day 1 (clean and scalable)

**Ubuntu 24.04 LTS**

- Docker + Docker Compose
- Coolify
- PostgreSQL (container)
- Redis (container)
- Uptime Kuma (container)
- (Optional) Portainer

Then add:

- OpenClaw (container) behind auth
- n8n (container) if you’re doing automations

---

## KVM 2 reality check (so you don’t overload it)

2 vCPU / 8 GB RAM is fine for:

- multiple marketing sites
- a couple web apps
- Postgres + Redis
- n8n
- basic monitoring

It is **not** ideal for heavy AI inference. For “agents” it’s fine if they call external APIs; not fine if you want to run big local models.

---

## Your first structure for domains (keeps it tidy)

- `ybasystems.com` → marketing site
- `app.ybasystems.com` → your main webapp
- `status.ybasystems.com` → Uptime Kuma
- `automations.ybasystems.com` → n8n
- `claw.ybasystems.com` → OpenClaw (locked down)
- `db` never public

---

If you want the fastest “give me the exact steps” path: I can lay out the **exact install order + commands** for Ubuntu 24.04 on Hostinger (SSH hardening → Docker → Coolify → first app → SSL → backups).

#### You said:

i need a stack that can host my web sidte landing page and build secure backands with apps andrun n8n automations for clients and run open claw i would like open claw to be my server manager and web app engineer powered by chat gpt

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Here’s a **minimal, scalable, safe** stack that does exactly what you described (landing page + secure backends/apps + n8n client automations + OpenClaw), without turning your VPS into a junk drawer.

## OS

- **Ubuntu Server 24.04 LTS**

## Core runtime

- **Docker**
- **Docker Compose**
- **Git**

## Edge layer (domains + HTTPS + routing)

Pick **one**:

- **Traefik** (best with many Docker apps), **or**
- **Caddy** (simplest)

## Deployment / “cPanel replacement”

Pick **one**:

- **Coolify** (recommended), **or**
- **Dokploy** (lighter alternative)

## Databases

- **PostgreSQL**
- **Redis**

## Automations (client workflows)

- **n8n**
- **n8n-worker setup** (only if you later scale executions; otherwise skip for now)

## Storage (files, uploads, backups, client docs)

- **S3-compatible storage**: **MinIO** (optional, but useful once apps start storing files)

## Monitoring

- **Uptime Kuma**
- **Netdata** (optional)

## Security baseline

- **UFW**
- **Fail2ban**
- **SSH keys only** (disable password login)
- **Automatic security updates** (unattended-upgrades)
- **Backups**: **Restic** (to an offsite location)

## OpenClaw (your “server manager / web app engineer”)

- **OpenClaw** (run as a Docker service)
- Put it **behind protection**:
	- **Cloudflare Access** *or* VPN/basic auth
		- **Do not expose it openly** on the internet

---

# What this stack hosts (cleanly)

### Landing page

- Host as:
	- Static site container (Next.js/React build) **or**
		- Simple Nginx static container

### Backends / web apps

- Each app runs as its own Docker service
- TLS and routing handled by Traefik/Caddy
- Auth + secrets managed via environment variables + vault-like practices (later)

### n8n client automations

- One n8n instance can host multiple clients (with strict separation by folders/credentials)
- Use Postgres for n8n DB
- Use Redis if you scale executions/queues later

### OpenClaw

- Runs as another container
- Use it to:
	- generate code
		- scaffold apps
		- manage tasks
		- assist with troubleshooting
- But you still keep **real control** via:
	- Git repos
		- Docker Compose
		- Coolify (deployment)

---

# The “do this, not that” rules

- **Don’t** expose Postgres/Redis to the public internet.
- **Don’t** run everything on one giant container.
- **Do** use subdomains for everything:
	- `ybasystems.com` (landing)
		- `app.ybasystems.com` (your SaaS)
		- `automations.ybasystems.com` (n8n)
		- `claw.ybasystems.com` (OpenClaw, locked down)
		- `status.ybasystems.com` (Uptime Kuma)

---

# Minimal stack summary (if you want the shortest list)

1. Ubuntu 24.04
2. Docker + Compose + Git
3. Traefik (or Caddy)
4. Coolify
5. PostgreSQL + Redis
6. n8n
7. OpenClaw (behind Cloudflare Access)
8. UFW + Fail2ban + Restic + Uptime Kuma

If you want, I’ll give you the **exact docker-compose layout** (folders + networks + subdomains) that keeps n8n, OpenClaw, and your apps separated properly and secure by default.

#### ChatGPT said:

Here’s the **right sequence** to set up your Hostinger KVM2 VPS for:

- landing pages (ybasystems + ybaflow)
- secure backends + apps
- n8n client automations
- OpenClaw (locked down)

I’m assuming you will use **Ubuntu 24.04 LTS + Docker + Coolify** (simplest scalable “control panel” style). Coolify’s installer supports Ubuntu LTS including 24.04. [Coolify](https://coolify.io/docs/get-started/installation?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

---

## Phase 0 — Before you touch DNS (today)

1. **In Hostinger hPanel**
	- Create VPS
		- Install **Ubuntu 24.04 LTS (Server)** (not Desktop)
		- Note your **public VPS IP**
2. From your PC:
	- Install an SSH client (Windows Terminal is fine)
		- Generate an SSH key (if you don’t already have one)

---

## Phase 1 — First login + hardening (do this first, before installing apps)

3. SSH into the VPS as root (Hostinger provides details)
4. **Update OS**
	- `apt update && apt upgrade -y`
5. **Create a non-root admin user**
	- add user
		- add to sudo group
6. **Enable SSH key login for that user**
7. **Disable SSH password login**
	- This is a core hardening step Hostinger also recommends. [Hostinger+1](https://www.hostinger.com/tutorials/how-to-disable-ssh-password-login-on-vps?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
8. **Firewall (UFW)**
	- Allow: `22/tcp`, `80/tcp`, `443/tcp`
		- Enable UFW
		- (Hostinger has a UFW guide if you want the reference.) [Hostinger](https://www.hostinger.com/tutorials/how-to-configure-firewall-on-ubuntu-using-ufw?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
9. Install **Fail2ban**
10. Enable **unattended-upgrades** (security updates)

At this point your server is safe enough to proceed.

---

## Phase 2 — Container platform (your “base layer”)

11. Install:
- **Docker**
- **Docker Compose plugin**
12. Create a folder convention (example):
- `/opt/stacks/`
- `/opt/backups/`

---

## Phase 3 — Install Coolify (deployment + HTTPS + routing)

13. Install **Coolify** using their recommended method (on Ubuntu LTS 24.04 it’s supported). [Coolify+1](https://coolify.io/docs/get-started/installation?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
14. Log into Coolify dashboard using the VPS IP and set admin account
15. In Coolify:
- Set server timezone
- Set default project structure (YBASystems / YBAFlow)

---

## Phase 4 — Databases first (so apps don’t become spaghetti)

16. In Coolify, create managed services (or Docker services):
- **PostgreSQL**
- **Redis**
17. Make sure these are **NOT publicly exposed** (no public ports).

---

## Phase 5 — Deploy your first “hello world” apps (still no DNS change)

18. Deploy a test landing page:
- simplest: static site container (Nginx) or Next.js
19. Deploy a test backend app:
- connect to Postgres/Redis internally

You can access these via:

- Coolify-provided domain/temporary URL OR
- direct IP + port during testing

---

## Phase 6 — Add n8n (client automations)

20. Deploy **n8n** (Docker)
21. Configure n8n to use:
- Postgres as its DB
- (Optional later) Redis/queue mode if you scale
22. Lock down n8n:
- strong admin credentials
- IP allowlist if possible
- put behind HTTPS via Coolify

---

## Phase 7 — Add OpenClaw (but locked down)

23. Deploy **OpenClaw** as a Docker app
24. **Do not expose it openly**
- Put it behind Cloudflare Access (best) or at minimum basic auth/VPN
- Treat it as an internal admin tool

---

## Phase 8 — Point your GoDaddy domains to the VPS (later, when you’re satisfied)

You said both domains are currently “hosted on GoDaddy” and you’ll point them later. The clean approach is:

### Option A (recommended): Keep DNS at GoDaddy, just change A records

25. In GoDaddy DNS, add/edit:
- `A` record for `@` → your VPS IP
- `A` record for `www` → your VPS IP
- `A` record for `app` → your VPS IP
- `A` record for `automations` → your VPS IP
- `A` record for `claw` → your VPS IP (but protected)  
	GoDaddy’s help doc for adding/editing A records is here. [GoDaddy](https://www.godaddy.com/help/add-or-edit-an-a-record-42545?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

### Option B: Change nameservers (only if you’re moving DNS elsewhere)

26. Change nameservers in GoDaddy (not required if you keep DNS there). [GoDaddy](https://www.godaddy.com/help/edit-my-domain-nameservers-664?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

---

## Phase 9 — Final routing layout (what you’ll end up with)

- `ybasystems.com` → landing page
- `ybaflow.com` → landing page or redirect to systems page
- `app.ybaflow.com` → your SaaS app
- `automations.ybasystems.com` → n8n
- `claw.ybasystems.com` → OpenClaw (behind Access/VPN)
- `status.ybasystems.com` → Uptime Kuma (optional, later)

---

- SSH hardening

# PHASE 1 — Access Your VPS

### 1\. Login to Hostinger

Go to  
[https://hpanel.hostinger.com](https://hpanel.hostinger.com)

Open:

**VPS → Your VPS → Manage**

You will see:

- **Server IP**
- **Username: root**
- **Password**

Write down the **IP address**.

---

# PHASE 2 — Connect to the Server

On Windows open **Windows Terminal** or **PowerShell**.

Run:

Bash

ssh root@YOUR\_SERVER\_IP

Example:

Bash

ssh root@185.123.45.67

When asked:

Are you sure you want to continue connecting?

Type:

yes

Then paste the **root password** from Hostinger.

You are now inside the server.

---

# PHASE 3 — Update the Server

First update everything.

Run:

Bash

apt update && apt upgrade \-y

This may take a minute.

---

# PHASE 4 — Create Your Admin User

Do not run servers as root.

Create your own user.

Example using **richard**:

Bash

adduser richard

Set password.

Then give admin rights:

Bash

usermod \-aG sudo richard

---

# PHASE 5 — Setup SSH Key Login (important)

On **your laptop**, open terminal and run:

Bash

ssh-keygen

Press enter for everything.

Then run:

Bash

ssh-copy-id richard@YOUR\_SERVER\_IP

Now login as your new user:

Bash

ssh richard@YOUR\_SERVER\_IP

---

# PHASE 6 — Disable Root Login

Open ssh config:

Bash

sudo nano /etc/ssh/sshd\_config

Change these lines:

PermitRootLogin no  
PasswordAuthentication no

Save:

CTRL + X  
Y  
Enter

Restart ssh:

Bash

sudo systemctl restart ssh

---

# PHASE 7 — Setup Firewall

Install firewall:

Bash

sudo apt install ufw \-y

Allow essential ports:

Bash

sudo ufw allow 22  
sudo ufw allow 80  
sudo ufw allow 443

Enable firewall:

Bash

sudo ufw enable

Check:

Bash

sudo ufw status

---

# PHASE 8 — Install Fail2Ban

Bash

sudo apt install fail2ban \-y

Start service:

Bash

sudo systemctl enable fail2ban  
sudo systemctl start fail2ban

---

# PHASE 9 — Install Docker

Run:

Bash

curl \-fsSL https://get.docker.com | sh

Add your user to docker group:

Bash

sudo usermod \-aG docker $USER

Reload session:

Bash

exit

Then reconnect:

Bash

ssh richard@YOUR\_SERVER\_IP

Test Docker:

Bash

docker run hello-world

---

# PHASE 10 — Install Docker Compose

Bash

sudo apt install docker-compose-plugin \-y

Test:

Bash

docker compose version

---

# PHASE 11 — Create Server Folder Structure

Run:

Bash

sudo mkdir \-p /opt/stacks  
sudo mkdir \-p /opt/backups

---

# PHASE 12 — Install Coolify (Your Server Control Panel)

Run:

Bash

curl \-fsSL https://cdn.coollabs.io/coolify/install.sh | sudo bash

Wait until installation finishes.

Coolify will start automatically.

---

### Why

| Option | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| **This Machine** | Use the VPS you just installed Coolify on |
| **Remote Server** | Connect another server via SSH |
| **Hetzner Server** | Auto-deploy servers from Hetzner cloud |

For your setup:

Select → This Machine

Then continue.

#### You said:

this is the notification i got lets fix this Server is not reachable Please check the connection details below and correct them if they are incorrect. Port 22 User root Non-root user is experimental: docs If the connection details are correct, please ensure: The correct public key is in your ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys file for the specified user Or skip the boarding process and manually add a new private key to Coolify and the server For more help, check this documentation. Current Public Key ssh-ed25519 AAAAC3NzaC1lZDI1NTE5AAAAIEpqPVo8TiEsM7pB5FL8TUt5xEPjcWJYr5zbvJH89NoJ Check Again

  

#### ChatGPT said:

That error is happening because Coolify is trying to SSH into the server as **root**, but on Ubuntu you likely already did (or Ubuntu defaults to) **root SSH blocked** / or you disabled it when hardening.

Fix is straightforward: **use a normal sudo user** and add Coolify’s public key to that user.

## Do this now (fastest safe fix)

### 1) SSH into the VPS the normal way (from your PC)

Use the user you created earlier (example `richard`):

Bash

ssh richard@YOUR\_SERVER\_IP

If you *don’t* have a non-root user, tell me and I’ll give the 3 commands to create one correctly.

---

### 2) Add Coolify’s public key to that user

Copy the “Current Public Key” Coolify shows (starts with `ssh-ed25519 AAAA...`).

On the VPS, run:

Bash

mkdir \-p ~/.ssh  
chmod 700 ~/.ssh  
echo 'PASTE\_COOLIFY\_PUBLIC\_KEY\_HERE' >> ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys  
chmod 600 ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys

Example (yours will differ):

Bash

echo 'ssh-ed25519 AAAAC3NzaC1lZDI1NTE5AAAAI...' >> ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys

---

### 3) Make sure your user can sudo without a password (Coolify needs this)

Run (replace `richard` with your username):

Bash

sudo bash \-lc 'echo "richard ALL=(ALL) NOPASSWD:ALL" > /etc/sudoers.d/richard-nopasswd && chmod 440 /etc/sudoers.d/richard-nopasswd'

---

### 4) In Coolify: change the connection user (important)

Go back to that “Server is not reachable” screen and change:

- **Port:** `22`
- **User:** change from `root` → `richard` (or whatever your sudo user is)

Then click **Check Again**.

---

## If it still says “not reachable”

On the VPS, run these and paste the output back here:

Bash

sudo systemctl status ssh \--no-pager | head \-n 20  
sudo ufw status

Also confirm: can you SSH from your PC successfully as your non-root user right now?

**Most likely fix:** Coolify is using `root` and root SSH is disabled — switching to your sudo user + adding the key solves it.

#### You said:

i clicked start Starting database. Creating directories. Directories created successfully. bash: line 11: /data/coolify/databases/x0goc4skwwsgcw4gw004sc0w/README.md: Permission denied

  

#### ChatGPT said:

That error is a **host filesystem permission** problem: Coolify is trying to write a small file into:

`/data/coolify/databases/<id>/README.md`

…but the `/data/coolify` path is not writable by the user/process doing the setup.

Fix the permissions on the host, then start the DB again.

## 1) SSH into the VPS and run these checks

Bash

sudo ls \-ld /data /data/coolify /data/coolify/databases  
sudo ls \-ld /data/coolify/databases/\* 2\>/dev/null | head

## 2) Apply the standard fix (safe and usually works)

This makes Coolify’s data directory writable.

Bash

sudo mkdir \-p /data/coolify/databases  
sudo chown \-R 1000:1000 /data/coolify  
sudo chmod \-R u+rwX,g+rwX /data/coolify

If that doesn’t work (rare), use the “force” fix below.

## 3) Restart Coolify services (so it retries cleanly)

Bash

sudo docker ps \--format "table {{.Names}}\\t{{.Status}}" | grep \-i coolify || true  
sudo docker restart coolify coolify-proxy 2\>/dev/null || true

(If the container names differ, don’t stress—just run `sudo docker ps` and restart the containers that include “coolify”.)

3. Image:

		- `WEBHOOK_URL=https://automations.ybasystems.com/`

	- Destination path: `/home/node/.n8n`

#### You said:

where would i fing public exposure

  

#### ChatGPT said:

In **Coolify**, “public exposure” is controlled by whether the service has a **domain / port exposed through the proxy**.

For **databases like PostgreSQL and Redis**, you normally **do nothing** and they remain **internal only**. That means they are **not publicly exposed**.

### How to confirm Redis is not public

When creating Redis:

1. Go to  
	**YBA → production → Add Resource → Database → Redis**
2. On the configuration screen check the section called something like:

Networking  
Ports  
Domains  
Expose

For Redis you should see **no domain field** and **no port exposed to the public**.

That means it is already **internal only**.

Internal services communicate through Docker networking like this:

n8n → redis  
n8n → postgres  
apps → postgres

They **do not need internet access**.

---

### Rule for your server

| Service | Public? |
| --- | --- |
| PostgreSQL | ❌ No |
| Redis | ❌ No |
| n8n | ✅ Yes |
| Landing pages | ✅ Yes |
| OpenClaw | ⚠️ Only if protected |

---

### When you WILL see public exposure

When you deploy things like:

n8n  
websites  
APIs  
apps

Coolify will ask for:

Domain  
Port  
HTTPS

That is when it becomes **public**.

---

### Your next step

Continue Redis deployment and click **Deploy**.

When Redis shows:

Running  
Healthy

tell me.

Then we will deploy **n8n correctly**, because there is **one very important setting** that prevents it from crashing on small VPS servers like yours.

  

#### You said:

SHA256 Digest (optional) what sould id do

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Leave **SHA256 Digest** **empty**.

### What it is

The SHA256 digest is an **exact fingerprint of a Docker image version**.  
It forces Docker to pull a **specific immutable build** instead of the latest tag.

Example:

n8nio/n8n@sha256:abcd1234....

This is mainly used for:

- strict production environments
- compliance / reproducible builds
- CI pipelines

### For your setup

You do **not need it**.

Use the normal image tag instead.

Example for n8n:

n8nio/n8n:latest

or

n8nio/n8n:1

### What you should do

Simply:

Leave SHA256 Digest blank

Then click:

Deploy

---

### Small tip (important later)

Once your system is stable, we usually **pin versions** like this:

n8nio/n8n:1.87.0

This prevents unexpected updates breaking your automations.

---

After Redis finishes deploying, tell me what status you see:

Running  
Healthy

Then we will set up **n8n properly with PostgreSQL** so your automations are reliable.

#### ChatGPT said:

Perfect — now we can fix it cleanly.

Key details from your output:

- Coolify container runs as **`www-data`**
- `/data/coolify/...` is owned by **`ubuntu:ubuntu`**  
	So Coolify can’t write its SSH keys there → deployment fails.

## Fix (do exactly)

### 1) Change ownership of Coolify data directory to www-data

Bash

sudo chown \-R www-data:www-data /data/coolify  
sudo chmod \-R 775 /data/coolify

### 2) Ensure the keys folder exists

Bash

sudo mkdir \-p /data/coolify/ssh/keys  
sudo chown \-R www-data:www-data /data/coolify/ssh  
sudo chmod \-R 775 /data/coolify/ssh

### 3) Quick write test (optional but useful)

Bash

sudo \-u www-data bash \-lc 'touch /data/coolify/ssh/keys/test && rm /data/coolify/ssh/keys/test && echo OK'

You want it to print: `OK`

### 4) Restart Coolify

Bash

docker restart coolify coolify-proxy coolify-sentinel coolify-realtime 2\>/dev/null || true

2026-Mar-05 14:48:01.408116 ======================================== 2026-Mar-05 14:48:01.412717 Deployment failed: SSH keys storage directory is not writable 2026-Mar-05 14:48:01.447585 ======================================== 2026-Mar-05 14:48:01.451241 Deployment failed. #### ChatGPT said:

Cool — we now know **what** is failing, but it’s still failing after ownership change, which means one of these is true:

1. Coolify is checking a **different path** than `/data/coolify/ssh/keys`, **or**
2. The directory is mounted into the container as **read-only**, **or**
3. Permissions didn’t actually change the way we think (or got reverted).

We’ll fix this by **proving which path Coolify is using** and making it writable.

## Step 1 — Run these 4 commands and paste the output

Run on the VPS:

Bash

sudo stat \-c "%A %U:%G %n" /data /data/coolify /data/coolify/ssh /data/coolify/ssh/keys  
sudo \-u www-data bash \-lc 'touch /data/coolify/ssh/keys/\_write\_test && rm /data/coolify/ssh/keys/\_write\_test && echo "WRITE\_OK"'  
docker inspect coolify \--format '{{range .Mounts}}{{println .Source "->" .Destination "(ro=" .RW ")"}}{{end}}'  
docker logs \--tail 120 coolify

What this tells us:

- **stat**: who owns the folders
- **write test**: can www-data actually write there
- **mounts**: whether `/data/coolify` is mounted **read-write**
- **logs**: Coolify usually prints the exact path it’s complaining about

## Step 2 — While you do that, apply a “force-open” permission temporarily

This is just to stop you getting stuck. We can tighten it later.

Bash

Then restart Coolify:

Bash

docker restart coolify coolify-proxy coolify-sentinel coolify-realtime 2\>/dev/null || true

#### ChatGPT said:

We’ve got the smoking gun.

Your `docker inspect` shows these mounts:

- `/data/coolify/ssh -> /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh (ro= true )`
- `/data/coolify/applications -> … (ro= true )`
- `/data/coolify/databases -> … (ro= true )`  
	…etc.

So inside the Coolify container, those folders are mounted **read-only**. No amount of `chown` will fix that. We must change Coolify’s Docker Compose so these mounts are **read-write**.

## Fix: edit Coolify’s docker-compose and flip ro → rw

### 1) Find Coolify’s compose file

Run:

Bash

sudo ls \-la /data/coolify/source  
sudo ls \-la /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml 2\>/dev/null || true  
sudo find /data/coolify/source \-maxdepth 2 \-type f \-iname "\*compose\*.yml" \-o \-iname "docker-compose.yml"

You should see a `docker-compose.yml` path (most installs use `/data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml`).

### 2) Edit the compose file

Open it:

Bash

sudo nano /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

Find the `coolify` service and look for volume lines like these (examples):

\- /data/coolify/ssh:/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh:ro  
\- /data/coolify/applications:/var/www/html/storage/app/applications:ro  
\- /data/coolify/databases:/var/www/html/storage/app/databases:ro  
\- /data/coolify/services:/var/www/html/storage/app/services:ro  
\- /data/coolify/backups:/var/www/html/storage/app/backups:ro

Change **`:ro` to `:rw`** (or remove the suffix entirely).

So they become:

\- /data/coolify/ssh:/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh:rw  
\- /data/coolify/applications:/var/www/html/storage/app/applications:rw  
\- /data/coolify/databases:/var/www/html/storage/app/databases:rw  
\- /data/coolify/services:/var/www/html/storage/app/services:rw  
\- /data/coolify/backups:/var/www/html/storage/app/backups:rw

Save:

- `CTRL + X`, then `Y`, then `Enter`

### 3) Recreate Coolify containers from the updated compose

Run:

Bash

cd /data/coolify/source  
sudo docker compose up \-d

### 4) Verify the mounts are now RW

Run:

Bash

docker inspect coolify \--format '{{range .Mounts}}{{println .Source "->" .Destination "(rw=" .RW ")"}}{{end}}'

You want to see `(rw= true)` for `/data/coolify/ssh`, `/applications`, `/databases`, etc.

### 5) Retry the deploy in Coolify UI

Now redeploy Redis / your resource. The “SSH keys storage directory not writable” error should be gone.

---

## If you don’t find `:ro` in the compose file

Paste the output of:

Bash

sudo sed \-n '1,220p' /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

(That’s safe—no passwords there unless you manually put them in. If you see secrets, stop and tell me before pasting.)

This is the correct fix because the container is literally mounted read-only right now.

#### ChatGPT said:

That snippet is only the **bottom of the compose file** (networks). The read-only mounts will be **inside the `services:` section**, higher up.

We need to find the lines that set those mounts to `:ro` and change them.

## 1) Find the read-only mounts in the compose file

Run:

Bash

sudo grep \-nE "(:ro|storage/app|/data/coolify/(ssh|applications|databases|services|backups))" /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

This will print the exact lines + line numbers.

## 2) Edit the file at those lines

Open the file:

Bash

sudo nano /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

In nano:

- Press **CTRL + W** (search)
- Search for `:ro`
- Every time you see something like:

/data/coolify/ssh:/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh:ro

Change `:ro` → `:rw` (or delete the suffix completely).

Do the same for:

- `/data/coolify/applications ... :ro`
- `/data/coolify/databases ... :ro`
- `/data/coolify/services ... :ro`
- `/data/coolify/backups ... :ro`

Save:

- **CTRL + X**, then **Y**, then **Enter**

## 3) Recreate Coolify with the updated compose

Bash

cd /data/coolify/source  
sudo docker compose up \-d

## 4) Confirm mounts are now writable

Bash

docker inspect coolify \--format '{{range .Mounts}}{{println .Source "->" .Destination "(rw=" .RW ")"}}{{end}}'

You want to see `rw= true` for the `/data/coolify/...` mounts.

## 5) Retry your deployment in Coolify UI

Now deploy Redis again (or whatever failed). The “SSH keys storage directory is not writable” error should be gone.

---

Don’t wipe the whole server. You only need to reset **the failed resource** (Redis/app) — your Coolify install itself is the thing that’s broken right now because its folders are mounted **read-only**. If you “start from scratch” without fixing that, you’ll hit the same error again.

Here’s the clean “reset” that actually works:

## A) Delete the failed resource in Coolify (safe)

In the Coolify UI:

1. Go to **YBA → production**
2. Click the failed resource (Redis / app)
3. Click **Delete resource**
4. When it asks about storage/volume:
	- **Keep storage** if it’s a database you might want to preserve
		- **Delete storage** only if you’re sure there is no valuable data (Redis usually has none you care about)

That resets the resource.

## B) Fix the real root cause (required)

Your Coolify container has these mounts as **read-only**:

- `/data/coolify/ssh -> … (ro=true)`
- `/data/coolify/databases -> … (ro=true)` etc.

Until we flip them to **rw**, deployments will keep failing.

Do this now:

### 1) Find the `:ro` entries

Bash

sudo grep \-n ":ro" /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

### 2) Edit the compose file and replace `:ro` with `:rw`

Bash

sudo nano /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

- Search `:ro` (CTRL+W)
- Replace with `:rw`
- Save (CTRL+X, Y, Enter)

### 3) Recreate Coolify containers

Bash

cd /data/coolify/source  
sudo docker compose up \-d

### 4) Confirm mounts are writable

Bash

docker inspect coolify \--format '{{range .Mounts}}{{println .Source "->" .Destination "(rw=" .RW ")"}}{{end}}'

You must see `rw= true` for `/data/coolify/ssh`, `/databases`, `/services`, etc.

Back in Coolify:

- **\+ Add Resource → Database → Redis → Deploy**

**Core**

NODE\_ENV=production  
N8N\_HOST=automations.ybasystems.com  
N8N\_PROTOCOL=https  
N8N\_PORT=5678  
WEBHOOK\_URL=https://automations.ybasystems.com/

**Encryption key (important)**  
Generate a long random value. If you want a quick one, run this on your VPS and copy it:

Bash

openssl rand \-hex 32

Then set:

N8N\_ENCRYPTION\_KEY=<paste the value>

**Database (PostgreSQL)**  
Use values from your Coolify PostgreSQL resource for host/user/password, but the variables must be exactly:

DB\_TYPE=postgresdb  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_HOST=<your-postgres-service-hostname>  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_PORT=5432  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_DATABASE=n8n  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_USER=<postgres-user>  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_PASSWORD=<postgres-password>

Tip: in Coolify the **DB host** is usually the service name of the postgres resource on the internal network (often something like `postgresql-xxxx`).

# What you just did

You created a database structure like this:

PostgreSQL  
├── postgres (default)  
└── n8n (for automations)

n8n will now store:

- workflows
- credentials
- execution logs
- webhook settings

inside that **n8n database**.

---

# Important (why we do this)

If we didn’t do this, n8n would use **SQLite**, which:

- breaks with multiple workers
- corrupts on crashes
- is not scalable

PostgreSQL is **production-grade**.

---

# Step 1 — Check if the `n8n` database exists

SSH into your VPS.

Run this command:

Bash

docker exec \-it coolify-db psql \-U postgres \-l

This tells PostgreSQL to **list all databases**.

You should see something like:

List of databases  
Name      | Owner  
\----------+---------  
postgres  | postgres  
template0 | postgres  
template1 | postgres  
n8n       | postgres

---

# Step 2 — If `n8n` does NOT exist

Create it with:

Bash

docker exec \-it coolify-db psql \-U postgres \-c "CREATE DATABASE n8n;"

Then verify again:

Bash

docker exec \-it coolify-db psql \-U postgres \-l

docker exec -it coolify-db psql -U postgres -l psql: error: connection to server on socket "/var/run/postgresql/.s.PGSQL.5432" failed: FATAL: role "postgres" does not exist

  

#### ChatGPT said:

You’re hitting the wrong Postgres.

`coolify-db` is **Coolify’s internal database container**, and in your install it’s not using a `postgres` role (it may be using a different user). Your **project PostgreSQL** (the one you created under **YBA → production**) is a different container.

So: find the **right Postgres container**, then list/create the `n8n` database there.

## 1) Find the correct PostgreSQL container name

SSH into the VPS and run:

Bash

docker ps \--format "table {{.Names}}\\t{{.Image}}\\t{{.Status}}" | grep \-i postgres

You should see **two** postgres containers:

- `coolify-db` (Coolify internal)
- another one (your YBA Postgres service)

Copy the **name** of the non-`coolify-db` one.

## 2) Find the DB username/password that container is using

Run (replace `YOUR_POSTGRES_CONTAINER` with the name you found):

Bash

docker inspect YOUR\_POSTGRES\_CONTAINER \--format '{{range .Config.Env}}{{println .}}{{end}}' | grep \-E '^POSTGRES\_(USER|PASSWORD|DB)='

You will get something like:

- `POSTGRES_USER=xxxx`
- `POSTGRES_PASSWORD=xxxx`
- `POSTGRES_DB=xxxx`

## 3) List databases (using the correct user)

Replace `DBUSER` with the value of `POSTGRES_USER`:

Bash

docker exec \-it YOUR\_POSTGRES\_CONTAINER psql \-U DBUSER \-l

## 4) If `n8n` database does not exist, create it

Bash

docker exec \-it YOUR\_POSTGRES\_CONTAINER psql \-U DBUSER \-c "CREATE DATABASE n8n;"

Re-check:

Bash

docker exec \-it YOUR\_POSTGRES\_CONTAINER psql \-U DBUSER \-l

At this point, `n8n` should appear in the list.

---

# Next: Deploy the n8n container (correctly)

When you’re ready, we will set n8n DB variables to match that same Postgres container:

DB\_TYPE=postgresdb  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_HOST=<Coolify internal hostname for your Postgres service>  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_PORT=5432  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_DATABASE=n8n  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_USER=<POSTGRES\_USER value>  
DB\_POSTGRESDB\_PASSWORD=<POSTGRES\_PASSWORD value>

#### You said:

at step 1 its asking volume mount file mount or directory mount

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Choose **Volume Mount**.

For n8n you want a **Docker volume**, not a file or directory mount.

### Why

- **Volume Mount** → managed by Docker, safest and easiest
- **File Mount** → only for single config files
- **Directory Mount** → advanced/manual host path mapping

For Coolify deployments the correct choice almost always is **Volume Mount**.

---

# What to do

Select:

Volume Mount

Then fill the fields like this.

### Destination Path

/home/node/.n8n

### Volume Name

You can leave the generated name or set:

n8n-data

Then click:

Save

---

When finished your storage section should look like something similar to:

Volume Name: n8n-data  
Destination Path: /home/node/.n8n

That ensures your:

- workflows
- credentials
- history
- API keys

are **not lost if the container restarts**.

#### ChatGPT said:

Use this as your `N8N_ENCRYPTION_KEY`:

73be0e497020a3633408c24eb795d1dd007722d88faebfcf0722e18428ddfd4f

The deployment failure is **not n8n-specific**. Coolify itself is still broken.

The real issue is:

SSH keys storage directory is not writable

Earlier we confirmed Coolify’s internal storage mounts were **read-only** inside the Coolify container. Until that is fixed, **any new application deployment can fail**, including n8n.

## Do this now on the server

Reconnect:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

### 1) Find the Coolify compose file lines that contain read-only mounts

Run:

Bash

grep \-n ":ro" /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

You should see lines with things like:

/data/coolify/ssh:/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh:ro  
/data/coolify/applications:/var/www/html/storage/app/applications:ro  
/data/coolify/databases:/var/www/html/storage/app/databases:ro  
/data/coolify/services:/var/www/html/storage/app/services:ro  
/data/coolify/backups:/var/www/html/storage/app/backups:ro

### 2) Edit the file

Open it:

Bash

sudo nano /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

Inside the file, replace every `:ro` with `:rw`.

So for example change:

/data/coolify/ssh:/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh:ro

to:

/data/coolify/ssh:/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh:rw

Do that for all the Coolify storage mounts.

Save with:

- `Ctrl + X`
- `Y`
- `Enter`

### 3) Restart Coolify from that compose file

Run:

Bash

cd /data/coolify/source  
sudo docker compose up \-d

### 4) Confirm the mounts are writable now

Run:

Bash

docker inspect coolify \--format '{{range .Mounts}}{{println .Source "->" .Destination "(rw=" .RW ")"}}{{end}}'

You want to see `rw= true` for:

- `/data/coolify/ssh`
- `/data/coolify/applications`
- `/data/coolify/databases`
- `/data/coolify/services`
- `/data/coolify/backups`

#### ChatGPT said:

Good. That result matters.

Bash

grep \-n ":ro" /data/coolify/source/docker-compose.yml

returned **nothing**, which means the earlier problem was **not** caused by `:ro` in the compose file.

Also, the previous mount output was misleading because the label printed `ro=` while the Docker field was actually `.RW`. That means we were reading it backwards.

So the next move is to test the **actual path inside the Coolify container**.

Run these commands on the server and paste the output:

Bash

docker exec coolify sh \-lc 'id && ls -ld /var/www/html/storage/app /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys && touch /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys/testfile && rm /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys/testfile && echo WRITE\_OK'

Then run:

Bash

docker exec coolify sh \-lc 'php -r "echo is\_writable(\\"/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh\\") ? \\"SSH\_WRITABLE\\n\\" : \\"SSH\_NOT\_WRITABLE\\n\\"; echo is\_writable(\\"/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys\\") ? \\"KEYS\_WRITABLE\\n\\" : \\"KEYS\_NOT\_WRITABLE\\n\\";"'

This will tell us exactly whether:

- the `www-data` user can write there
- the `keys` directory exists
- PHP itself sees it as writable

If you want the likely fix in advance, it will probably be one of these:

Bash

sudo mkdir \-p /data/coolify/ssh/keys  
sudo chown \-R www-data:www-data /data/coolify/ssh  
sudo chmod \-R 775 /data/coolify/ssh  
docker restart coolify

But run the two test commands first so we fix the right thing.

#### ChatGPT said:

Found it.

Inside the Coolify container:

- process runs as **uid 9999 / gid 9999**
- the SSH folders are owned by **uid 33 / gid 33**

So Coolify cannot write there.

## Fix it now

Run these commands on the server:

Bash

sudo chown \-R 9999:9999 /data/coolify/ssh  
sudo chmod \-R 775 /data/coolify/ssh  
docker restart coolify

Then test again:

Bash

docker exec coolify sh \-lc 'id && ls -ld /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys && touch /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys/testfile && rm /var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys/testfile && echo WRITE\_OK'

WRITE\_OK

docker exec coolify sh \-lc 'php -r "echo is\_writable(\\"/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh\\") ? \\"SSH\_WRITABLE\\n\\" : \\"SSH\_NOT\_WRITABLE\\n\\"; echo is\_writable(\\"/var/www/html/storage/app/ssh/keys\\") ? \\"KEYS\_WRITABLE\\n\\" : \\"KEYS\_NOT\_WRITABLE\\n\\";"'

That means the domain is resolving, but **the SSL certificate is not valid yet**.

Most likely causes:

1. **Coolify has not issued the Let’s Encrypt certificate yet**
2. The app is still serving a **default/self-signed certificate**
3. DNS has not fully propagated when Coolify tried to issue the cert

## Do this in order

### 1) Confirm DNS points correctly

On your VPS, run:

Bash

You want to see:

### 2) Check the domain inside Coolify

You want to see that:

- `automations.ybasystems.com` is attached to the app

- certificate status is not failed

If there is a button like **Generate SSL**, **Force HTTPS**, or **Redeploy**, use it.

### 3) Redeploy once more

After DNS is confirmed, redeploy the n8n app again in Coolify.

This often triggers the correct certificate request.

### 4) Wait a few minutes

Let’s Encrypt issuance is not always instant. Give it about **5–10 minutes** after DNS is correct.

### 5) Test in an incognito window

Open:

https://automations.ybasystems.com

If Chrome still shows the warning, click the certificate details and check whether it says:

- self-signed
- default Traefik cert
- expired
- wrong domain

## Very likely outcome

You are currently getting either:

- the **default Traefik certificate**
- or a **self-signed certificate**

That usually means Coolify has not yet completed Let’s Encrypt for that hostname.

#### ChatGPT said:

You now have the exact cause. There are **two separate problems**.

## Problem 1: Traefik cannot issue Let’s Encrypt certs

Your log says:

unable to get ACME account: permissions 775 for /traefik/acme.json are too open, please use 600

That alone is enough to stop valid certificates from being issued.

## Problem 2: The domain rule in Coolify is malformed

Your log also says:

Host(\`\`) && PathPrefix(\`automations.ybasystems.com\`)

That is wrong.

It means Coolify has a **broken domain/path entry** for the n8n app.  
`automations.ybasystems.com` must be a **domain**, not a path prefix.

---

# Fix this in order

## Step 1 — Fix Traefik ACME file permissions

On your server, run:

Bash

docker inspect coolify-proxy \--format '{{range .Mounts}}{{println .Source "->" .Destination}}{{end}}'

You are looking for the host path that maps to:

/traefik/acme.json

It will likely show something like:

/data/coolify/proxy/acme.json -> /traefik/acme.json

Once you see the real host path, run:

Bash

sudo chmod 600 THE\_REAL\_HOST\_PATH\_TO\_ACME\_JSON  
sudo chown 9999:9999 THE\_REAL\_HOST\_PATH\_TO\_ACME\_JSON 2\>/dev/null || true  
docker restart coolify-proxy

Replace `THE_REAL_HOST_PATH_TO_ACME_JSON` with the actual path from the inspect output.

---

## Step 2 — Fix the broken domain entry in Coolify

Go to your **n8n app** in Coolify.

Open the **General** tab and check the **Domains** section.

You need it to contain only a proper hostname, like:

automations.ybasystems.com

Remove anything broken, especially if you see anything like:

- blank host
- path prefix
- slash-based route
- duplicate domain entries
- the old `sslip.io` domain if it is conflicting

The n8n app should have **one clean domain only**:

automations.ybasystems.com

Save it.

---

## Step 3 — Make sure the domain field is not being used as a path

The log strongly suggests someone or something put this in the wrong place:

automations.ybasystems.com

as a **path**, not a **host/domain**.

So in the app settings, check if there is any field like:

- Path
- Path Prefix
- Base Path
- Direction / Path routing

If you see `automations.ybasystems.com` in any of those, remove it.

That value belongs only in the **domain/host** field.

---

## Step 4 — Redeploy n8n

After fixing the domain entry, redeploy the n8n app.

---

## Step 5 — Check Traefik logs again

Run:

Bash

docker logs coolify-proxy \--tail 100

You want the following errors to disappear:

- `permissions 775 for /traefik/acme.json are too open`
- `Host(\`\`) && PathPrefix(...)\`
- `Router uses a nonexistent certificate resolver`

---

# Most likely final working state

## In Coolify n8n General tab

Use:

- **Domain:** `automations.ybasystems.com`
- **Exposed Port:** `5678`
- **No path prefix**
- **No duplicate temp domain** if it conflicts

## In n8n env vars

Use:

N8N\_HOST=automations.ybasystems.com  
WEBHOOK\_URL=https://automations.ybasystems.com/  
N8N\_PROTOCOL=https

That part is already right.

---

or

In the **n8n app → General**, make sure:

Good. That means the ACME file is inside:

/data/coolify/proxy

The file Traefik cares about is almost certainly:

/data/coolify/proxy/acme.json

## Do this now

Run these commands on the server:

Bash

sudo ls \-l /data/coolify/proxy  
sudo touch /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json  
sudo chmod 600 /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json  
sudo chown 9999:9999 /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json 2\>/dev/null || sudo chown root:root /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json  
docker restart coolify-proxy

## Then verify the permissions

Run:

Bash

ls \-l /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json

You want to see permissions like:

\-rw------- 

That is the key part.

---

## Then fix the malformed domain rule in Coolify

Your logs showed this broken rule:

Host(\`\`) && PathPrefix(\`automations.ybasystems.com\`)

So in the **n8n app** inside Coolify, go to the **General** area and check the routing/domain fields carefully.

It must be:

- **Domain / Host:** `automations.ybasystems.com`
- **Path / Path Prefix:** empty
- remove the old `sslip.io` domain if it is still attached
- remove any blank or duplicate domain entries

You should end with **one clean domain only**:

automations.ybasystems.com

Then **Save**.

#### ChatGPT said:

You’re close. The logs show the real blocker now:

Host(\`\`) && PathPrefix(\`automations.ybasystems.com\`)

That is a **bad Traefik rule**. It means your n8n app is still configured with:

- an **empty host**
- and `automations.ybasystems.com` placed as a **path prefix**

That is why you get:

- **503**
- **TRAEFIK DEFAULT CERT**
- no valid Let’s Encrypt cert

The ACME permission issue may also still exist, but the malformed router rule is definitely still active.

## Fix this in the UI first

Go to your **n8n app** in Coolify and check these fields carefully.

### General tab

Use only this:

- **Domains:** `automations.ybasystems.com`
- **Ports Exposes:** `5678`

And make sure these are empty:

- **Port Mappings**
- **Custom Docker Options**
- **Pre-deployment**
- **Post-deployment**
- any **Path** or **Path Prefix** field if it exists

If you see any route/path field containing:

automations.ybasystems.com

delete it. That value must only be in the **Domains** field.

---

## The fastest way to confirm what Coolify is sending to Traefik

Run this on the server:

Bash

docker ps \--format "table {{.Names}}\\t{{.Image}}" | grep n8nio/n8n

It will show the actual n8n container name.

Then run, replacing `N8N_CONTAINER_NAME` with that name:

Bash

docker inspect N8N\_CONTAINER\_NAME \--format '{{json .Config.Labels}}'

Paste that output here.

I want to see the Traefik labels, especially anything like:

- `traefik.http.routers...rule`
- `traefik.http.services...loadbalancer.server.port`

That will show exactly where the bad `Host('') && PathPrefix(...)` is coming from.

---

## Also verify ACME file permissions again

Run:

Bash

ls \-l /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json

You want to see:

\-rw-------

If not, run:

Bash

sudo chmod 600 /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json  
docker restart coolify-proxy

---

## What I think is happening

Right now there is almost certainly **one bad field left in Coolify**, probably one of these:

- an empty hostname entry
- a path/prefix field containing `automations.ybasystems.com`
- a stale old route attached to the same app

The logs keep repeating the same malformed rule, so we need to inspect the container labels directly.

Run these two commands and paste both outputs:

Bash

docker ps \--format "table {{.Names}}\\t{{.Image}}" | grep n8nio/n8n  
ls \-l /data/coolify/proxy/acme.json

#### ChatGPT said:

Found it.

Your container labels prove the exact misconfiguration:

traefik.http.routers...rule = Host(\`\`) && PathPrefix(\`automations.ybasystems.com\`)  
caddy\_0 = ://  
caddy\_0.handle\_path = automations.ybasystems.com\*

That means **Coolify did not read `automations.ybasystems.com` as a domain**.  
It read it as a **path**.

## The fix

In **Coolify → n8n → General → Domains**, do **not** enter just:

automations.ybasystems.com

Enter the **full URL**:

https://automations.ybasystems.com

That is the key fix.

Your earlier generated temp domain was shown as:

http://vk8s4ww4ow8gw04ckoccksgk.31.187.72.210.sslip.io

So this field expects a **full URL**, not only a hostname.

---

https://automations.ybasystems.com

### 3) Make sure these are still empty

- **Port Mappings**
- **Custom Docker Options**
- **Pre-deployment**
- **Post-deployment**

### 4) Save

### 5) Redeploy n8n

## Why this happened

Coolify’s **Domains** field for this app expects a URL-style value.  
Because you entered only the hostname, it built a broken route.

Change it to:

https://automations.ybasystems.com

and that should unblock both routing and Let’s Encrypt.

no longer exists.

Unlike n8n, OpenClaw should be protected.

Minimum protection:

- Cloudflare Zero Trust later

For now, easiest is:

- deploy it
- enable **HTTP Basic Authentication** in Coolify

On your VPS, keep OpenClaw modest.

Use roughly:

- CPU: `0.5`

- Max memory: `1GB`

That keeps enough room for:

- PostgreSQL
- Redis
- n8n
- OpenClaw

It is **not** good for heavy local AI models.

So OpenClaw should call:

- OpenAI API
- Claude API

Not run big models locally.

## Important decision

### Option A (Recommended)

Run OpenClaw **locally on your laptop**, not on the server.

SSH

Git

Run OpenClaw **inside the server**.

Problems:

- security risk
- harder debugging
- limited compute
- circular dependency (AI managing itself)

I generally only recommend this **after the system is stable**.

## Why laptop-first is the right move

When OpenClaw runs on your laptop, it can still control the server through:

- **SSH**
- **Git**
- **Docker commands**
- **API access**
- **file editing in your repos**

So it can still:

- write code
- update your apps
- help fix configs
- push changes to Git
- deploy through Coolify
- inspect logs
- connect to the VPS

That gives you most of the power you want **without giving an AI agent direct permanent residency inside production**.

## Why this is safer

If OpenClaw lives on the server and has broad control, then a mistake can affect:

- your live websites
- n8n
- databases
- reverse proxy
- SSL
- backups

That is too much authority too early.

From your laptop, OpenClaw becomes a **controlled operator**, not a resident superuser.

You can still let it do real work, but with a better control chain:

OpenClaw on laptop  
→ edits code / configs  
→ pushes to Git  
→ deploys to VPS  
→ you validate

That is far safer than:

OpenClaw on VPS  
→ directly changes production  
→ breaks routing / SSL / containers

## Will it still be efficient?

Yes, if you structure it properly.

The efficient setup is:

### On your laptop

- OpenClaw
- Claude Code
- Git
- local development tools

### On your VPS

- Coolify
- PostgreSQL
- Redis
- n8n
- websites
- apps
- monitoring
- backups

### Connection between them

- SSH key access
- GitHub or Git server
- Docker/Coolify deployment workflow

That lets OpenClaw:

- build apps locally
- review configs
- write deployment files
- help troubleshoot production
- manage infrastructure changes in a controlled way

## What it can do well from your laptop

Very well:

- build landing pages
- scaffold web apps
- write backend code
- create Dockerfiles
- create compose files
- help manage domains and deployment configs
- review logs from the VPS
- update n8n workflows through APIs or files
- build monitoring and backup systems

Less ideal:

- constantly acting as a real-time autonomous production operator 24/7
- directly running server-side background agent tasks all day
- local-model heavy workloads on weak hardware

But for what you described, **laptop-first is absolutely viable**.

## Best long-term model

The smart progression is:

### Phase 1

**OpenClaw on laptop**

- development
- server management assistance
- deployment help

### Phase 2

Once your infrastructure is stable:

- create a **staging server**
- test OpenClaw automation there first

### Phase 3

Only later, if truly needed:

- run a controlled server-side agent with limited permissions

That is how you stay efficient **and** avoid self-inflicted outages.

## Direct answer

Yes, you can do this efficiently and safely from your laptop **if you treat the laptop as the control plane and the VPS as the production plane**.

That is the right setup for where you are now.

The wrong move would be putting OpenClaw directly in production before your platform, backups, monitoring, and deployment discipline are mature.

The next decision is straightforward:

- If you want the **safest and smartest route**, choose **A**
- If you want the **most dangerous but most autonomous route**, choose **B**

You should choose **A** right now.

Press Enter through defaults.

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

If it logs in, good.

## Save server shortcut

On your laptop, edit or create:

C:\\Users\\YOURNAME\\.ssh\\config

Add:

ssh

Host yba-vps  
    HostName 31.187.72.210  
    User richard

Then you can connect with:

Bash

ssh yba-vps

Much cleaner.

#### You said:

ok wait would it not be betterif i created i virtual machien for safety or a container to protect open claw from hackers or what would you or is your plan just as safe

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Yes — a **VM or isolated container is better** than running it directly on your daily-use laptop OS.

My earlier plan is safe enough to start, but **your revised idea is better** if you want OpenClaw to have meaningful system access while reducing risk.

## Best practical setup

Use this structure:

Laptop  
├─ Main OS  
│  └─ your normal work  
└─ Isolated environment  
   └─ OpenClaw / dev tools  
        ├─ Git  
        ├─ SSH keys  
        ├─ project files  
        └─ controlled access to VPS

## What I recommend

### Best option for you

Use a **dedicated Linux VM** on your laptop.

That gives you:

- isolation from your main machine
- easier snapshot/rollback
- cleaner dev environment
- safer SSH key separation
- lower risk if OpenClaw misbehaves or a dependency is compromised

## Why VM is better than just a container

A **container is lighter**, but for your use case a **VM is safer** because:

- OpenClaw may need filesystem access
- you may install dev tools, agents, browsers, CLIs
- containers share the host kernel
- a VM gives much stronger isolation

So for **security-first local AI operator**, I would choose:

VM > container > host machine directly

## What I would do in your case

### Option 1 — Best balance

**Windows laptop + Ubuntu VM**

Inside the VM:

- Git
- VS Code / code-server or editor
- OpenClaw
- Claude Code
- SSH key just for server access
- project folders for YBA

This is the cleanest route.

### Option 2 — Advanced later

VM plus Docker inside the VM.

That gives you:

- one isolated mini-dev-server
- reproducible environment
- even cleaner tooling separation

That is probably the strongest setup for you.

## What not to do

Do **not**:

- run OpenClaw directly on your production VPS yet
- give OpenClaw your main personal SSH keys
- mix it into your everyday laptop environment with unrestricted access

## Safe access model

Even inside the VM, keep permissions controlled.

Use:

- a **dedicated SSH key**
- a **non-root VPS user**
- `sudo` only when needed
- Git-based deployment wherever possible

So OpenClaw works like this:

OpenClaw in VM  
→ edits code/config  
→ commits to Git  
→ SSH to VPS when needed  
→ deploys deliberately

That is a strong model.

## My actual recommendation

For you, the best answer is:

**Yes, create a dedicated Ubuntu VM and run OpenClaw there.**

That is better than my earlier “plain laptop” suggestion.

My earlier plan was good operationally, but your VM idea is **safer and more professional**.

## Practical build order

1. Create Ubuntu VM on laptop
2. Install Git, VS Code, Node, SSH tools inside VM
3. Create dedicated SSH key inside VM
4. Give that key access to VPS
5. Build `ybasystems-site` and `ybaflow-site` from inside VM
6. Add OpenClaw inside VM
7. Let it assist development and controlled server management

## Direct answer

Yes — a VM is the better call.

A container is acceptable, but a **VM is safer** for your use case.

So the refined plan is:

**Laptop main OS → Ubuntu VM → OpenClaw inside VM → VPS via SSH/Git**

That is the route I would choose.

The next step is deciding **which VM tool you want to use on your laptop** so we can set it up correctly.

Short answer: **Do not use that Hostinger “OpenClaw hosting” setup for your current architecture yet.** It is designed for a different use case than what you are building.

Let’s break it down clearly.

---

# What that Hostinger page is actually selling

The page is essentially promoting:

**“Run OpenClaw directly on a VPS.”**

Architecture they assume:

Internet  
   │  
OpenClaw (running on VPS)  
   │  
AI tools / automation / agents

Meaning the AI agent lives **inside the server** and operates there.

That is convenient for beginners but **not the safest architecture for a production platform** like yours.

---

# Your current architecture (better)

Right now you have:

Laptop / Dev machine  
   │  
OpenClaw + dev tools  
   │  
SSH / Git  
   │  
VPS  
 ├─ Coolify  
 ├─ PostgreSQL  
 ├─ Redis  
 ├─ n8n  
 └─ future apps

This is called **control plane vs production plane** separation.

It is the safer model used by real infrastructure teams.

---

# Why their approach is risky for you

If OpenClaw runs directly on the VPS:

OpenClaw  
 ├─ Docker access  
 ├─ filesystem access  
 ├─ environment secrets  
 ├─ database access  
 └─ root permissions

One mistake or prompt issue could:

- stop Coolify
- delete containers
- break SSL
- expose secrets
- destroy your n8n database

That risk is **not theoretical**. It happens frequently with AI agents that have unrestricted system access.

---

# Why your VM idea is stronger

Your idea of using a **VM on your laptop** creates a safe boundary:

Laptop  
 └─ Ubuntu VM  
     └─ OpenClaw  
  
Server  
 └─ Production infrastructure

If OpenClaw makes a mistake:

- it only affects the VM
- not your server
- not your production data

You simply revert the VM snapshot.

---

# The best long-term architecture

What you are building should evolve like this:

### Phase 1 (now)

Laptop VM  
 └─ OpenClaw + dev tools  
  
VPS  
 ├─ Coolify  
 ├─ n8n  
 ├─ apps  
 └─ databases

Safe and controlled.

---

### Phase 2 (later)

Add a **staging server**:

Laptop OpenClaw  
      │  
      ▼  
Staging VPS  
      │  
      ▼  
Production VPS

OpenClaw can automate staging first.

---

### Phase 3 (advanced)

Eventually you might run a **limited agent service** on the server itself.

But with strict permissions.

---

# When the Hostinger OpenClaw VPS setup would make sense

That page is useful if someone is:

- building a **public AI agent service**
- running OpenClaw as a **SaaS product**
- hosting an **agent platform**

You are not doing that.

You are using OpenClaw as an **internal development assistant**.

So their architecture is not the best fit.

---

# Verdict

| Option | Safety | Control | Recommendation |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| OpenClaw on VPS (Hostinger page) | Low | High risk | ❌ Not now |
| OpenClaw on laptop | Good | Good | ✔ Acceptable |
| OpenClaw inside laptop VM | Very good | Best | ✔✔ Recommended |

Your instinct about **VM isolation was correct**.

---

## On the VM question: with your laptop specs, be careful

Your laptop is roughly:

- **i7**
- **16 GB RAM**
- **RTX 3080 laptop GPU**
- Lenovo Y740 17"

The main constraint here is **RAM**, not the GPU.

A VM is still possible, but with **16 GB RAM** you do **not** want a heavy Ubuntu VM running all day while also using Chrome, Cursor, Docker, and other tools on Windows.

## My judgment

### Cursor

**Yes, use Cursor.**

### Full-time VM

**Possible, but not ideal** on 16 GB RAM if you want a smooth experience.

# Best option for your actual hardware

For your machine, I would **not** start with a full VM first.

I would do this instead:

## Option 1 — Best balance for you now

Run **Cursor directly on Windows**, but isolate the risky parts.

Use:

- **Cursor on Windows**
- **Git on Windows**
- **SSH keys dedicated only to YBA**
- optionally **WSL2 Ubuntu** for Linux tooling
- no full VM yet

This gives you most of the safety and flexibility without crushing your RAM.

Architecture:

Windows laptop  
├─ Cursor  
├─ Git  
├─ dedicated SSH key for VPS  
└─ WSL2 Ubuntu (optional, recommended)

This is lighter than a VM and still clean enough.

---

# Why WSL2 is probably the sweet spot

For your hardware, **WSL2 is better than a full VM** right now.

Benefits:

- much lighter on RAM
- Linux shell available
- good for Git, SSH, Node, Docker tooling
- easier than managing a separate VM
- works well with Cursor

So instead of:

Windows → VirtualBox/VMware → Ubuntu VM

I would do:

Windows → Cursor  
Windows → WSL2 Ubuntu  
Windows/WSL2 → SSH to VPS

That is a strong setup for your laptop.

---

# Safety question: is this safe enough?

Yes, if you do it properly.

The real safety comes less from “VM vs no VM” and more from **access control**.

## To make your setup safe

Do these things:

### 1\. Use a dedicated SSH key only for YBA

Not your personal everything-key.

### 2\. Use a non-root VPS user

You already have `richard`, which is good.

### 3\. Prefer Git-based changes

Let Cursor build/edit locally, then push and deploy.  
Do not let tools freestyle inside production.

### 4\. Keep production secrets off your general desktop where possible

Use `.env` discipline and separate project folders.

### 5\. Add backups before giving any AI too much freedom

That matters more than VM purity.

---

# What I would recommend for you specifically

## Right now

Use:

- **Cursor**
- **Git**
- **WSL2 Ubuntu**
- **dedicated YBA folder**
- **dedicated SSH key**
- **VPS as production**
- no OpenClaw on VPS

## Later, if needed

If you upgrade to **32 GB RAM**, then a proper Ubuntu VM becomes much more attractive.

With 32 GB, I would tell you to do the VM immediately.

With **16 GB**, I think a VM is possible but inefficient.

# Step 1 — Install Cursor (10 minutes)

Download:

[https://cursor.sh](https://cursor.sh)

Install normally on Windows.

After installation:

1. Open Cursor
2. Sign in with your account
3. Enable **terminal inside Cursor**

You will use Cursor as the main editor.

---

# Step 2 — Install WSL2 Ubuntu (10 minutes)

Open **PowerShell as Administrator** and run:

PowerShell

wsl \--install

This installs:

- WSL2
- Ubuntu
- Linux kernel

After installation, **restart your laptop** if prompted.

Then open **Ubuntu** from the start menu.

---

# Step 3 — Update Ubuntu (2 minutes)

Inside Ubuntu run:

Bash

sudo apt update && sudo apt upgrade \-y

---

# Step 4 — Install core development tools (5 minutes)

Inside Ubuntu run:

Bash

sudo apt install git curl build-essential \-y

Then install Node LTS:

Bash

curl \-fsSL https://deb.nodesource.com/setup\_lts.x | sudo \-E bash \-  
sudo apt install nodejs \-y

Verify:

Bash

node \-v  
npm \-v

---

# Step 5 — Create your YBA workspace (3 minutes)

Inside Ubuntu:

Bash

mkdir \-p ~/YBA  
cd ~/YBA

Create project folders:

Bash

mkdir ybasystems-site  
mkdir ybaflow-site  
mkdir server-config

Check:

Bash

ls

You should see:

ybasystems-site  
ybaflow-site  
server-config

---

# Step 6 — Create a dedicated SSH key for the VPS (5 minutes)

Inside Ubuntu:

Bash

ssh-keygen \-t ed25519 \-C "yba-control"

Press enter through defaults.

Now show the public key:

Bash

cat ~/.ssh/id\_ed25519.pub

Copy the output.

---

# Step 7 — Add the key to your VPS

SSH to your VPS:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

Then run:

Bash

mkdir \-p ~/.ssh  
nano ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys

Paste the public key on a new line.

Save with:

CTRL + X  
Y  
Enter

Then secure permissions:

Bash

chmod 700 ~/.ssh  
chmod 600 ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys

---

# Step 8 — Test passwordless login

Back in WSL:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

If it logs in **without asking for a password**, it's correct.

---

# Step 9 — Create the YBA website project (10 minutes)

Inside WSL:

Bash

cd ~/YBA/ybasystems-site

Initialize git:

Bash

git init

Create a simple landing page:

Bash

nano index.html

Paste:

HTML

<!DOCTYPE html>  
<html>  
<head>  
<title>YBA Systems</title>  
<meta charset\="UTF-8"\>  
<style>  
body{  
font-family:Arial;  
background:#0f172a;  
color:white;  
display:flex;  
justify-content:center;  
align-items:center;  
height:100vh;  
text-align:center;  
}  
h1{font-size:60px}  
p{font-size:22px;color:#94a3b8}  
</style>  
</head>  
<body>  
  
<div>  
<h1>YBA Systems</h1>  
<p>Automation Infrastructure</p>  
<p>Powered by YBAFlow</p>  
</div>  
  
</body>  
</html>

Save.

---

# Step 10 — Deploy it with Coolify (15 minutes)

In Coolify:

Add Resource  
Application  
Docker Image

Use this image:

nginx:alpine

Port:

80

Domain:

https://ybasystems.com

Storage: none required.

---

# Then upload the page

SSH to server:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

Find the nginx container:

Bash

docker ps

Copy the container name.

Then copy your file:

Bash

scp ~/YBA/ybasystems-site/index.html richard@31.187.72.210:/tmp

Inside server:

Bash

docker cp /tmp/index.html CONTAINERNAME:/usr/share/nginx/html/index.html

---

You will have:

https://ybasystems.com

live with a landing page.

That is a **real deliverable**.

im at step 6 yba@LAPTOP-EJD15RVM:~/YBA$ ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "yba-control" Generating public/private ed25519 key pair. Enter file in which to save the key (/home/yba/.ssh/id\_ed25519): Created directory '/home/yba/.ssh'. Enter passphrase (empty for no passphrase):

  

#### ChatGPT said:

Good. You’re exactly where you should be.

You now need to decide about the **passphrase**. This matters for security vs convenience.

## What I recommend for you

Since this key will control your **production VPS**, use a passphrase.

Type a passphrase you can remember. Example structure:

3–4 random words + number

Example (do NOT use this exact one):

orchard-silver-bridge-42

When you type it, nothing will appear on the screen — that is normal.

You will then see:

Enter passphrase (empty for no passphrase):  
Enter same passphrase again:

Enter it twice.

After that you should see something like:

Your identification has been saved in /home/yba/.ssh/id\_ed25519  
Your public key has been saved in /home/yba/.ssh/id\_ed25519.pub

## Then immediately run this

Bash

cat ~/.ssh/id\_ed25519.pub

It will output something like:

ssh-ed25519 AAAAC3NzaC1lZDI1NTE5AAAA... yba-control

Copy that entire line.

Do **not share it publicly**, but it is safe to paste into the server.

## Next step (2 minutes)

We will add that key to your VPS so you can log in **without passwords**.

Tell me once you see the `.pub` key printed.

  

#### You said:

ssh-ed25519 AAAAC3NzaC1lZDI1NTE5AAAAILVLbCs/qf26nVptOkSDhMEqsp0C18Fx4ID/JZZO7rSJ yba-control here is that code We will add that key to your VPS so you can log in without passwords. Tell me once you see the .pub key printed. i dont know what this is

  

#### ChatGPT said:

What you pasted is **your public SSH key**. That’s expected. It’s safe to place on the server so the server knows “this laptop is allowed to log in.”

Right now the situation is:

- **Private key (secret):** `~/.ssh/id_ed25519` → stays on your laptop only
- **Public key:** `~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub` → gets added to the server’s `authorized_keys`

We will now tell your VPS to trust your laptop.

---

# Step 7 — Add your public key to the VPS

From your WSL terminal run:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

Enter your password one last time.

---

## Then run this on the VPS

Open the authorized keys file:

Bash

mkdir \-p ~/.ssh  
nano ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys

You will see a blank file.

Paste your key exactly:

ssh-ed25519 AAAAC3NzaC1lZDI1NTE5AAAAILVLbCs/qf26nVptOkSDhMEqsp0C18Fx4ID/JZZO7rSJ yba-control

Then save:

CTRL + X  
Y  
Enter

---

# Step 8 — Secure the SSH folder

Still on the VPS run:

Bash

chmod 700 ~/.ssh  
chmod 600 ~/.ssh/authorized\_keys

---

# Step 9 — Test passwordless login

Exit the server:

Bash

exit

Then from WSL run:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

You should now see:

Enter passphrase for key '/home/yba/.ssh/id\_ed25519':

Enter the passphrase you created earlier.

If it logs in **without asking for your server password**, the setup is correct.

# 2\. Connect Cursor to WSL (important)

Cursor works best when editing files **inside WSL**, not Windows.

In Cursor press:

Ctrl + Shift + P

Search:

Remote: Connect to WSL

Select your Ubuntu instance.

Cursor will reload and show something like:

WSL: Ubuntu

in the bottom left corner.

This is important because now:

Cursor → Linux filesystem → Git/Node tools

#### You said:

Connect Cursor to WSL (important) when i search i cant find remote connect wsl

  

#### ChatGPT said:

That’s normal. Cursor does **not use the same “Remote-WSL” extension system as VS Code**, so you will not find **“Remote: Connect to WSL.”** Cursor handles WSL differently.

The correct method is simpler.

---

# Correct way to use Cursor with WSL

Instead of connecting to WSL, you **open the WSL filesystem directly**.

## Step 1 — Open your Linux home folder

In Cursor:

File → Open Folder

Then navigate to:

\\\\wsl$\\Ubuntu\\home\\yba

If your WSL username is different, adjust accordingly.

You should see something like:

\\\\wsl$\\Ubuntu\\home\\yba

Select the folder:

YBA

and open it.

---

# Step 2 — Verify the terminal is Linux

Now open the terminal inside Cursor:

Ctrl + \`

Run:

Bash

pwd

You should see something like:

/home/yba/YBA

NOT something like:

C:\\Users\\

If you see `/home/...` then Cursor is **operating inside Linux**, which is exactly what we want.

---

# Step 3 — Verify tools inside WSL

Run these commands:

Bash

node \-v  
git \--version  
python3 \--version

Tell me the results.

If any are missing we will install them.

---

# Step 4 — Verify SSH works from WSL

Run:

Bash

ssh richard@31.187.72.210

If you connect successfully, exit again:

Bash

exit

Now your laptop can control the server directly.
